        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504"><meta property="og:title" content="Construction of an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries with an Ultrastable Structure"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/acs.langmuir.4c03504.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="The reserves of sodium resources are much larger than those of lithium resources, and they are widely distributed and easy to produce and can be widely used in photovoltaic energy storage and other industries on the premise of this advantage. However, how to produce hard carbon anodes at a low cost for the preparation of energy storage materials requires continuous exploration and experimentation to find the optimal solution. Here, we used waste sour date shell biomass as a precursor for a hard carbon anode obtained by simple acid treatment and two pyrolyses. It is shown that acid washing after prepyrolysis has a significant effect on the electrochemical performance of the sour date shell-derived hard carbon (ZJ), constructing a stable structure that makes it easier for sodium ions to be embedded and dislodged, and the carbon particles are homogeneous and free of bonding and electrode cracking. The ZJ-1500-HCl pyrolyzed at 1500 °C has a high reversible capacity of 329.1 mAh g–1, 94.1% initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), 90.54% capacity retention efficiency cycling 300 cycles at a current density of 300 mA g–1, and a resistivity below 10 Ω. It has good cycle stability and a good multiplier performance. It is expected to be used in practical production and photovoltaic energy storage in the future."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/acs.langmuir.4c03504.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187e05c8a4075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187e05c8a4075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '2e546ba0-3963-4d3e-bd19-273a4a3aaf1f'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187e05c8a4075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'langmuir';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/langmuir/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/langmuir/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facs.langmuir.4c03504&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Langmuir</i></span><span <script nonce="93187e05c8a4075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "langd5";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Construction of an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries with an Ultrastable Structure", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Construction of an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries with an Ultrastable Structure", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Construction of an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries with an Ultrastable Structure", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Construction of an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries with an Ultrastable Structure", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Construction of an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries with an Ultrastable Structure", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Construction of an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries with an Ultrastable Structure", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Construction of an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries with an Ultrastable Structure", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Construction of an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries with an Ultrastable Structure", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Construction of an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries with an Ultrastable Structure", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val">-</div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/medium/la4c03504_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/large/la4c03504_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">The reserves of sodium resources are much larger than those of lithium resources, and they are widely distributed and easy to produce and can be widely used in photovoltaic energy storage and other industries on the premise of this advantage. However, how to produce hard carbon anodes at a low cost for the preparation of energy storage materials requires continuous exploration and experimentation to find the optimal solution. Here, we used waste sour date shell biomass as a precursor for a hard carbon anode obtained by simple acid treatment and two pyrolyses. It is shown that acid washing after prepyrolysis has a significant effect on the electrochemical performance of the sour date shell-derived hard carbon (ZJ), constructing a stable structure that makes it easier for sodium ions to be embedded and dislodged, and the carbon particles are homogeneous and free of bonding and electrode cracking. The ZJ-1500-HCl pyrolyzed at 1500 °C has a high reversible capacity of 329.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, 94.1% initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), 90.54% capacity retention efficiency cycling 300 cycles at a current density of 300 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, and a resistivity below 10 Ω. It has good cycle stability and a good multiplier performance. It is expected to be used in practical production and photovoltaic energy storage in the future.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D24644%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLe%2BWang%252C%2BYefeng%2BFeng%252C%2BYu%25E2%2580%2599an%2BLin%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D40%26issueNum%3D46%26contentID%3Dacs.langmuir.4c03504%26title%3DConstruction%2Bof%2Ban%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%2Bwith%2Ban%2BUltrastable%2BStructure%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0743-7463%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dlangd5%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D24652%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties"> Electrical properties </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions"> Ions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials"> Materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium"> Sodium </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As the world continues to develop, the demand for energy is increasing. However, oil and coal are nonrenewable energy sources. Consequently, there has been a significant focus on researching and developing new green and sustainable energy sources in recent years with the aim of ensuring a sustainable future for energy. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> Lithium-ion batteries occupy a central position in the power energy sector, such as electric vehicles and renewable energy storage, due to their superior energy density, long cycle life, and excellent power output capability. However, as global demand for lithium-ion batteries grows exponentially, the scarcity of lithium resources has become a notable challenge. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 5 to 9">(5−9)</a> Sodium batteries, as a part of green and sustainable energy, have lower production costs, and even the edible salt we eat contains a large amount of sodium. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 10 to 12">(10−12)</a> It also has excellent performance, is safer than lithium-ion batteries, and may be applied to photovoltaic energy storage and other fields in the future. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 13 to 15">(13−15)</a> Commonly used anode materials for sodium batteries mainly include hard carbon, which is also called amorphous carbon, has a large pore size and layer spacing, and is suitable for sodium ion exfoliation and embedding. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Biomass hard carbon comes from a wide range of sources, mainly trees, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17−19)</a> fruit shells, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 20 to 26">(20−26)</a> sugar, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> and so on. The use of such raw materials to prepare battery negative electrodes, on the one hand, recycle these waste biomasses, and on the other hand, reduce the emission of carbon dioxide, which is conducive to the protection of the environment. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The treatment methods of biomass hard carbon are also very simple, mainly the template method, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> pore-making method, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref32" aria-label="reference 20 and 32">(20,32)</a> coating method, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> and so on. Good energy density anode materials can be obtained by simple treatments, such as the high temperature method with acid washing, which is a natural advantage to be able to be used for large volume photovoltaic energy storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> However, the development and application of hard carbon anode materials prepared from biomass as precursors are seriously hampered by their low first-time coulometric efficiency, low cycle retention efficiency, and poor multiplicity performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">The sour jujube tree is widely distributed throughout China, and its fruits are of great value for food and medicinal purposes. However, the sour jujube shell (the kernel of the fruit of the sour jujube tree) is a waste product, and a large number of sour jujube shells are produced every year, and direct combustion releases a large amount of carbon dioxide and other pollutant gases, and some of them are used as fertilizers, with a low utilization rate. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In this paper, a method of adding an appropriate amount of citric acid to sour date shells as a precursor to prepare sodium battery negative electrode material is to uniformly mix a certain proportion of citric acid in sour date shells and then make it into negative electrode material by simple high-temperature heating and acid washing. This method greatly improves the first Coulomb efficiency, multiplication performance, and cycle retention efficiency. It solves the problems of low first coulomb efficiency and easy decay of biomass hard carbon anode materials and builds a stable charging and discharging structure, which is promising to be used in actual production in the future.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">ZJ-1500-HCl was prepared by using a sourdaw husk as the carbon source. The sour date husk was pounded into powder using a grinder. Then the powder was mixed with 4:1 citric acid in a ball mill and ball milled at 600 rpm for 12 h. The resulting powder was dried for 48 h and then preheated and solubilized at 600 °C under an argon gas flow at a temperature increase rate of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup> for 3 h. The powder was stirred with 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> HCl for 24 h at 60 °C in a constant-temperature magnetic stirrer and then rinsed with deionized water, filtered, and dried. It was then carbonized at 1500 °C under an argon gas flow at a ramp rate of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup> for 3 h. The resulting hard carbon material was named ZJ-1500-HCl. ZJ-1500 was prepared according to the above procedure, except for acid washing.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Structural Analysis</h3><div class="NLM_p">The preparation process of citric acid porous date shell hard carbon is shown in (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Lower carbonization temperatures result in fewer closed pores and higher carbonization temperatures result in less interlayer spacing, which is important for obtaining materials with better properties. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> To find the right carbonization temperature we used three different carbonization temperatures, 1300, 1500, and 1600 °C. In the end we found that a carbonization temperature of 1500 °C gave the best performing material (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). In order to remove the irreversible loss of capacity of the anode material due to inorganic impurities in sour date shells, the raw material was precharred at 600 °C and then treated with HCl; the resulting product was noted as ZJ-1500-HCl. Additionally, a sample of carbonized material at 1500 °C without HCl treatment (designated as ZJ-1500) was prepared for comparison. In order to investigate the surface morphology of ZJ-1500-HCl, electron microscopy (SEM) was carried out. The SEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) show that the material has a large number of pores and a large layer spacing, which facilitates the de-embedding of sodium ions.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to derive the pyrolysis process, thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed on ZJ-1500-HCl in air. The TGA curve (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) shows a decrease below 450 °C due to water gasification in the carbon material and a sharp decrease at 450–650 °C due to oxidation of carbon to gas. Biomass material is rich in cellulose and lignin, and high temperature pyrolysis converts it into bio-oil, where bio-oil high temperature pyrolysis produces a large amount of oxygen. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The carbon content of ZJ-1500-HCl is approximately 97.93% by TGA analysis. The material has a very high carbon yield with few impurities, most of which can be removed by high temperature pyrolysis.</div><div class="NLM_p">The XRD spectra of the ZJ-1500 and ZJ-1500-HCl samples are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a. The (002) peak at 23.5° and the (100) peak at 43° can be observed in the XRD plots of ZJ-1500 and ZJ-1500-HCl. The (002) peak of ZJ-1500 is located at a higher angle, which corresponds to a lower layer spacing for ZJ-1500-HCl. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The XRD spectra of both materials do not show a large number of impurity peaks,  there are few impurities after the treatment of sour date shells, and the curve of carbon purity becomes higher and smoother after pickling, which shows that pickling can effectively remove some impurities; it also shows that sour date shells are very suitable for hard carbon negative electrodes. The layer spacing was calculated according to Scherrrer’s formula, as shown in (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>), the layer spacing of ZJ-1500-HCl is 0.3748 nm greater than that of ZJ-1500, 0.3682 nm, which is more favorable for the de-embedding of sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/medium/la4c03504_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/large/la4c03504_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns of ZJ-1500-HCl and ZJ-1500. (b) FTIR spectra of ZJ-1500-HCl and ZJ-1500. (c) Raman spectra of ZJ-1500-HCl and ZJ-1500. (d) Adsorption–desorption isotherms of ZJ-1500-HCl and ZJ-1500. (e) Pore size distribution of ZJ-1500-HCl. (f) Pore size distribution of ZJ-1500. (g, h) HRTEM images of ZJ-1500-HCl. (i) HRTEM images of ZJ-1500. (j–l) Elemental mapping images of ZJ-1500-HCl.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/large/la4c03504_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The FTIR spectra of ZJ-1500 and ZJ-1500-HCl are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b. There are mainly an −OH functional group at 2996–3712 cm<sup>–1</sup>, a C═C functional group at 1642 cm<sup>–1</sup>, and a C–O functional group at 1083 cm<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> Compared with ZJ-1500, the peak intensities of −OH and C–O were significantly higher in ZJ-1500-HCl, indicating that the higher oxygen content of the acid-washed samples increased the defects and provided more adsorption sites for sodium storage, leading to the improvement of the specific capacity, and in addition, the higher electronegativity of oxygen ions also allowed the adsorption of more sodium ions, further improving the reversible capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">Raman spectra of ZJ-1500 and ZJ-1500-HCl samples show the main disordered defect structure of carbon in the D band near 1347 cm<sup>–1</sup> and the crystal structure of carbon in the G band near 1597 cm<sup>–1</sup> in the carbon material, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c. The integrated peak area ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio) increased from 1.610 for ZJ-1500 to 1.707 for ZJ-1500-HCl, indicating that the degree of disorder in ZJ-1500-HCl is superior to that of ZJ-1500. This shows that the structure of the acid-washed material is more complex than graphite, which can increase the active sites, adsorb more sodium ions, and improve the reversible capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The BET adsorption and desorption isotherm plots for the whole pore tests of ZJ-1500 and ZJ-1500-HCl are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d. The specific surface area of ZJ-1500-HCl (13.285 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) was slightly smaller than that of ZJ-1500 (13.706 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), and after the high temperature treatment, part of the open pores became closed, resulting in a lower specific surface area. It can be explained that the specific surface area of both materials is very small, and the lower specific surface area allows the electrolyte to have less contact with the negative electrode and a larger wetting angle, which inhibits the growth of the SEI film, fewer side reactions, and less sodium ions consumed, leading to high first-time Coulombic efficiencies. The adsorption isotherms of both materials exhibit type II characteristics, characterized by a substantial number of micropores and mesopores in the surface material. These observations are in accordance with the adsorption behavior of conventional high-temperature-treated hard carbon microporous materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e and f illustrate the pore size distribution (DFT full pore test) of ZJ-1500-HCl and ZJ-1500, respectively. The results demonstrate that both hard carbon materials are predominantly composed of micropores and mesopores, thereby facilitating electrolyte penetration and sodium ion de-embedding. Furthermore, the pore volume distribution was found to be more concentrated following the acid treatment. The results of the study show that the specific surface area of hard carbon materials can be effectively reduced by an acid washing treatment while increasing their pore diameter. This structural change helps to reduce the number of irreversible sodium ion storage sites present on the surface of the material. This means that the acid-washed hard carbon material, when used as an anode in sodium-ion batteries, can improve its electrochemical performance, particularly the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency, by reducing the irreversible loss of sodium ions during the first embedding process.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The micromorphology of the samples analyzed by high-resolution transmission electron microscopy is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g–i. Comparing ZJ-1500 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>i) and ZJ-1500-HCl (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g,h), ZJ-1500-HCl has a more disordered distribution of pores; in contrast, ZJ-1500 shows a more regular arrangement of parallel graphite-like stripes, which is more inclined to graphite morphology. A graphite-like morphology with a large number of nanovoids is observed in ZJ-1500-HCl, which is consistent with the results of previous analyses. According to the elemental mapping of ZJ-1500-HCl (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>j–l), it can be found that the material has a uniform ratio of carbon and oxygen elements, which is conducive to the stability of the structure as well as the embedding and detachment of sodium ions. The layer spacing of ZJ-1500 is 0.37 nm, and after hydrochloric acid treatment, the layer spacing of ZJ-1500-HCl is increased to 0.375 nm, which is very close to that of the layer spacing calculated by XRD. The increase in the layer spacing of ZJ-1500-HCl is favorable to the intercalation storage of Na<sup>+</sup>. It can be seen that the treated material has lower impurities, more disordered pore distribution, and larger layer spacing, which provides more internal channels for sodium ion transport.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">The performance of the ZJ-1500 and ZJ-1500-HCl was evaluated in a coin half battery system. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a illustrates the initial constant current charge–discharge curve at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. ZJ-1500-HCl was used as the electrolyte for the preparation of button cell batteries with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME and 1 M NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> in DME, and the results indicated that 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME electrolyte gave better results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). This is due to the suitability of the electrolyte, which has better electrochemical properties. We have also done different temperatures for anode materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>), with the best performance after heat treatment at 1500 °C.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/medium/la4c03504_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/large/la4c03504_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Electrochemical performance of ZJ-1500 and ZJ-1500-HCl in button half batteries. (a) First charge/discharge cycle at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> current density. (b) Cycling at different multiplicative current densities. (c) Charge/discharge cycle at 300 mA g<sup>–1</sup> current density. (d) Charge/discharge cycle at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/large/la4c03504_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The curves for both materials demonstrate the potential zones of a classical carbon material anode, namely, the inclined zone at high potentials and the plateau zone at low potentials. The first-charge capacities of 298.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for ZJ-1500 and 329.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for ZJ-1500-HCl, and the ICEs of 92.24 and 94.1%, respectively, are superior to those of the majority of reported carbon-based anodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The application of hydrochloric acid treatment serves to remove impure components from the feedstock, thereby altering the microstructure of  hard carbon, including the defect structure. The increase in defects contributes to the specific capacity of ZJ-1500-HCl, due to the high first Coulombic efficiency observed for both materials, despite the smaller specific surface area. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b illustrates the specific capacities of both materials at varying current densities spanning from 20 to 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. As the current density increases from 20  to 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacity of the materials declines gradually. However, the specific capacity of the acid-washed treated material decreases at a slower rate. This can be compared to the enhanced multiplicity performance of ZJ-1500-HCl, which exhibits a less pronounced decline in specific capacity with rising current density. The specific capacities of ZJ-1500 are 330.6, 307.8, 302.3, 294.6, 279.8, 270.6, 236.3, 198.1, and 130.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 20, 30, 60, 120, 240, 300, 600, 900, and 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">In comparison, the specific charging capacities of ZJ-1500-HCl at current densities of 20, 30, 60, 120, 240, 300, 600, 900, and 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> were 383.4, 376, 361.1, 339.8, 321.5, 312.1, 294.5, 282.1, and 263.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c illustrates the high current cycling performance of ZJ-1500 and ZJ-1500-HCl. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c illustrates the high-current cycling performance of ZJ-1500 and ZJ-1500-HCl, while <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d depicts the low-current cycling performance of the aforementioned electrodes. At a current of 300 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacity retention of ZJ-1500-HCl is 90.54% after 300 cycles, whereas the initial reversible capacity of ZJ-1500 is 201 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which subsequently decreases to 39.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles with a capacity retention of 17.15%. The acid-washed material displays enhanced specific capacity and capacity retention efficiency. The performance improvement exhibited by ZJ-1500 during the 1st to 11th cycles can be attributed to the high current density activation effect. This suggests that the high current during the initial cycling stage contributes to the full activation of the active sites within the material, which in turn improves the electrochemical reaction activity of the material.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In addition, the ZJ-1500-HCl sample showed a period of increasing performance after 66 cycles, indicating that  sufficient contact and reaction states between the active material and the electrolyte had been achieved. This phenomenon suggests that after a certain number of cycles, the active material has achieved full wetting and saturation in the electrolyte, which may trigger further optimization of the material properties. This may be due to the fact that both the surface and internal pores of the active material are fully penetrated by the electrolyte, making the embedding/desynthesizing process of the sodium ions more efficient, ultimately leading to an improvement in the electrochemical properties. ZJ-1500-HCl exhibited excellent cycling performance, with a superior capacity retention that exceeded that of the majority of reported hard carbon anodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>).</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Interfacial Chemical Analysis</h3><div class="NLM_p">The composition of ZJ-1500-HCl was analyzed by XPS. The fitted C 1s XPS peaks at 284.8, 286.7, and 289.3 eV correspond to C–C, C–O, and C═O, respectively, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b, the peaks of the fitted O 1s XPS peaks at 533.2 eV are phenol groups in biomass hard carbon, and the peak at 532.2 eV is carboxyl groups in biomass hard carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The peaks at 535.9 and 531.9 eV represent O–F and C═O functional groups, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c, the fitted F 1s XPS peak at 688 eV is a P–F functional group, and the peak at 685 eV is a Na–F functional group. From the C 1s, O 1s, and F 1s spectra, it can be seen that ZJ-1500-HCl has a high carbon content and a large number of oxygen-containing functional groups. In addition, the oxygen-containing functional groups can greatly increase the defect structure, and the oxygen ions have electronegativity to attract sodium ions, thus improving the reversible specific capacity. The electrolyte 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> has been embedded in the negative electrode with very stable functional groups, and the high inorganic composition and high F-fraction allow for rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport at the negative electrode/electrolyte interface. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/medium/la4c03504_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/large/la4c03504_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) C 1s acquired on ZJ-1500-HCl electrode, ZJ-1500-HCl electrode after direct discharge to 0.01 V (D-0.01 V), ZJ-1500-HCl electrode after direct charging to 3.0 V (C-3.0 V), and ZJ-1500-HCl electrode after cycling for 300 cycles at 0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) O 1s and (c) F 1s. (d) SEM images of ZJ-1500-HCl after cycling for 300 cycles at 0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (e, f) HRTEM images of ZJ-1500-HCl after 300 cycles at 0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (g) SEM images of ZJ-1500 after 300 cycles at 0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and (h, i) HRTEM image of ZJ-1500 after 300 cycles at 0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/asset/images/large/la4c03504_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The morphological characteristics of the electrodes of ZJ-1500-HCl (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d) and ZJ-1500 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g) were analyzed by SEM after cycling for 300 cycles at a current density of 0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The acid-washed carbon particles were uniform and dispersed with a rich mesoporous structure, which was suitable for the embedding and detachment of sodium ions. The unacid-washed ones have a large number of net structures, which hinder the embedding and detachment of sodium ions. The morphology of ZJ-1500-HCl (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e,f) and ZJ-1500 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h,i) was analyzed by HR-TEM after 300 cycles of ZJ-1500-HCl at a current density of 0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the thickness of the acid-washed SEI film was 2.66 nm, with a complete organization and uniform carbon distribution. The thickness of the nonacid-washed SEI film is 3.55 nm with cracks and carbon buildup in a mass. This corresponds to the SEM, where the thinner SEI film has a higher first time Coulombic efficiency, the uniform distribution of the carbon particles facilitates the de-embedding of sodium ions, and the cracks are the primary factor leading to the significant decrease of the specific capacity after 140 cycles, which is consistent with the above specific capacity diagram.</div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Dynamic Analysis</h3><div class="NLM_p">Resistance represents a crucial parameter of the cell. To investigate the resistance change of the ZJ-1500-HCl anode during sodium embedding and adsorption, we employed a classical model (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). The electrochemical impedance spectra of ZJ-1500-HCl are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a. ZJ-1500-HCl exhibits minimal resistance values at varying cycling states (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Compared with ZJ-1500-HCl, ZJ-1500 is not stable (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c03504/suppl_file/la4c03504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Prior to and following the formation of the SEI membrane, the resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>) generated by the SEI was observed to be minimal, while the interfacial charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) remained stable. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> This indicates that the SEI membrane of the ZJ-1500-HCl electrode has an excellent structure, which allows for rapid sodium ion transport and stable cycling. This conclusion is consistent with the HRTEM image of the pole piece, which was measured earlier. The sodium ion diffusion coefficient was determined by the GITT method <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> with a retention time of 1 h and a relaxation time of 15 min at a current density of 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. rect(3.128em, 1001.48em, 4.435em, -999.997em); top: -3.293em; left: 50%; margin-left: -0.736em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-67"><span class="msub" id="MathJax-Span-68"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.48em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.128em, 1000.68em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-69"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-70" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">Δ</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.804em; left: 0.741em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-71"><span class="msub" id="MathJax-Span-72"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.685em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.355em, 1000.51em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-73"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-74" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝐸<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span></span><span id="MathJax-Span-76" style="font-size: 50%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">t<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0em;"></span></span></span><span
